	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Barn swallow</h1>

</td></tr>

</tbody></table>
<p>The <b>barn swallow</b> (<i>Hirundo rustica</i>) is the most widespread species of <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallow</a> in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It is a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> bird with blue upperparts and a long, deeply forked <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a>. It is found in Europe, Asia, Africa and the Americas. In <a href="/wiki/English-speaking_world" title="English-speaking world">Anglophone</a> Europe it is just called the <b>swallow</b>; in Northern Europe it is the only common species called a "<a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallow</a>" rather than a "<a href="/wiki/Martin_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin (bird)">martin</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-GillAndWright_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GillAndWright-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are six subspecies of barn swallow, which breed across the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>. Four are strongly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a>, and their wintering grounds cover much of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> as far south as central <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a> of South Africa, and northern Australia. Its huge range means that the barn swallow is not endangered, although there may be local population declines due to specific threats.
</p><p>The barn swallow is a bird of open country that normally uses man-made structures to breed and consequently has spread with human expansion. It builds a cup <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nest</a> from mud pellets in barns or similar structures and feeds on insects caught in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This species lives in close association with humans, and its insect-eating habits mean that it is tolerated by humans; this acceptance was reinforced in the past by superstitions regarding the bird and its nest. There are frequent cultural references to the barn swallow in literary and religious works due to both its living in close proximity to humans and its annual migration.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The barn swallow is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_birds" title="List of national birds">national bird</a> of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>.
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barn_Swallow_Range.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Barn_Swallow_Range.png/220px-Barn_Swallow_Range.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Barn_Swallow_Range.png/330px-Barn_Swallow_Range.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Barn_Swallow_Range.png/440px-Barn_Swallow_Range.png 2x" data-file-width="3718" data-file-height="1861" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barn_Swallow_Range.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reported range from observations submitted to <a href="/wiki/EBird" title="EBird">eBird</a> shows the migration pattern of the species<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend" style="text-align:left;"><span class="legend-color" style="border: 1px solid gray; background-color:#0cfc0c; color:black; -webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;">&#160;</span>&#160;Year-round range</div><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend" style="text-align:left;"><span class="legend-color" style="border: 1px solid gray; background-color:#fafa11; color:black; -webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;">&#160;</span>&#160;Summer range</div><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959"/><div class="legend" style="text-align:left;"><span class="legend-color" style="border: 1px solid gray; background-color:#21fafa; color:black; -webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;">&#160;</span>&#160;Winter range</div></div></div></div>
<p>The adult male barn swallow of the nominate subspecies <i>H. r. rustica</i> is 17–19&#160;cm (6.7–7.5&#160;in) long including 2–7&#160;cm (0.79–2.76&#160;in) of elongated outer tail feathers. It has a wingspan of 32–34.5&#160;cm (12.6–13.6&#160;in) and weighs 16–22&#160;g (0.56–0.78&#160;oz). It has steel blue upperparts and a <a href="/wiki/Rufous" title="Rufous">rufous</a> forehead, chin and throat, which are separated from the off-white underparts by a broad dark blue breast band. The outer tail feathers are elongated, giving the distinctive deeply forked "swallow tail". There is a line of white spots across the outer end of the upper tail.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The female is similar in appearance to the male, but the tail streamers are shorter, the blue of the upperparts and breast band is less glossy, and the underparts paler. The juvenile is browner and has a paler rufous face and whiter underparts. It also lacks the long tail streamers of the adult.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The song of the male barn swallow is a cheerful warble, often ending with <i>su-seer</i> with the second note higher than the first but falling in pitch. Calls include <i>witt</i> or <i>witt-witt</i> and a loud <i>splee-plink</i> when excited (or trying to chase intruders away from the nest).<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Alarm_call" class="mw-redirect" title="Alarm call">alarm calls</a> include a sharp <i>siflitt</i> for predators like cats and a <i>flitt-flitt</i> for <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">birds of prey</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Hobby_(bird)" title="Hobby (bird)">hobby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This species is fairly quiet on the wintering grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The distinctive combination of a red face and blue breast band render the adult barn swallow easy to distinguish from the African <i>Hirundo</i> species and from the <a href="/wiki/Welcome_swallow" title="Welcome swallow">welcome swallow</a> (<i>Hirundo neoxena</i>) with which its range overlaps in <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In Africa the short tail streamers of the juvenile barn swallow invite confusion with juvenile <a href="/wiki/Red-chested_swallow" title="Red-chested swallow">red-chested swallow</a> (<i>Hirundo lucida</i>), but the latter has a narrower breast band and more white in the tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Barlow_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The barn swallow was described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a> as <i>Hirundo rustica</i>, characterised as "<i>H. rectricibus, exceptis duabus intermediis, macula alba notatîs</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <i>Hirundo</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word for "swallow"; <i>rusticus</i> means "of the country".<sup id="cite_ref-name_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This species is the only one of that genus to have a range extending into the Americas, with the majority of <i>Hirundo</i> species being native to Africa. This genus of blue-backed swallows is sometimes called the "barn swallows".<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GillAndWright_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GillAndWright-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> dates the English <a href="/wiki/Common_name" title="Common name">common name</a> "barn swallow" to 1851,<sup id="cite_ref-OED_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> though an earlier instance of the <a href="/wiki/Collocation" title="Collocation">collocation</a> in an English-language context is in Gilbert White's popular book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_History_and_Antiquities_of_Selborne" title="The Natural History and Antiquities of Selborne">The Natural History of Selborne</a></i>, originally published in 1789:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The swallow, though called the chimney-swallow, by no means builds altogether in chimnies &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93;, but often within barns and out-houses against the rafters&#160;... In <i>Sweden</i> she builds in barns, and is called <i>ladusvala</i>, the barn-swallow.<sup id="cite_ref-white_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>This suggests that the English name may be a <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a> on the Swedish term.
</p><p>There are few <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> problems within the genus, but the red-chested swallow—a resident of West Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo basin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>—was formerly treated as a subspecies of barn swallow. The red-chested swallow is slightly smaller than its migratory relative, has a narrower blue breast-band, and (in the adult) has shorter tail streamers. In <a href="/wiki/Bird_flight" title="Bird flight">flight</a>, it looks paler underneath than barn swallow.<sup id="cite_ref-Barlow_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barlow-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>Six subspecies of barn swallow are generally recognised. In eastern Asia, a number of additional or alternative forms have been proposed, including <i>saturata</i> by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ridgway" title="Robert Ridgway">Robert Ridgway</a> in 1883,<sup id="cite_ref-ssp_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssp-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <i>kamtschatica</i> by <a href="/wiki/Benedykt_Dybowski" title="Benedykt Dybowski">Benedykt Dybowski</a> in 1883,<sup id="cite_ref-Dickinson_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <i>ambigua</i> by <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Stresemann" title="Erwin Stresemann">Erwin Stresemann</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and <i>mandschurica</i> by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Meise" title="Wilhelm Meise">Wilhelm Meise</a> in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-ssp_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssp-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Given the uncertainties over the validity of these forms,<sup id="cite_ref-Dickinson_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dickinson-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> this article follows the treatment of Turner and Rose.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>H. r. rustica</i>, the nominate European subspecies, breeds in Europe and Asia, as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, south to North Africa, the Middle East and <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, and east to the <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei River</a>. It migrates on a broad front to winter in Africa, <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The barn swallows wintering in southern Africa are from across Eurasia to at least 91°E,<sup id="cite_ref-safring_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-safring-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and have been recorded as covering up to 11,660&#160;km (7,250&#160;mi) on their annual migration.<sup id="cite_ref-euring2_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euring2-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The nominate European subspecies was the first to have its genome sequenced and published.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>H. r. transitiva</i> was described by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hartert" title="Ernst Hartert">Ernst Hartert</a> in 1910. It breeds in the Middle East from southern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and is partially resident, though some birds winter in East Africa. It has orange red underparts and a broken breast band.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>H. r. savignii</i>, the resident <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptian</a> subspecies, was described by <a href="/wiki/James_Francis_Stephens" title="James Francis Stephens">James Stephens</a> in 1817 and named for French <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Marie_Jules_C%C3%A9sar_Savigny" title="Marie Jules César Savigny">Marie Jules César Savigny</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Decker_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Decker-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It resembles <i>transitiva</i>, which also has orange-red underparts, but <i>savignii</i> has a complete broad breast band and deeper red hue to the underparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>H. r. gutturalis</i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Antonio_Scopoli" title="Giovanni Antonio Scopoli">Giovanni Antonio Scopoli</a> in 1786,<sup id="cite_ref-ssp_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssp-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> has whitish underparts and a broken breast band. Breast chestnut and lower underparts more pink-buff.<sup id="cite_ref-rasmussen_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rasmussen-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The populations that breed in the central and eastern <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> have been included in this subspecies,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> although the primary breeding range is Japan and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>. The east Asian breeders winter across tropical Asia from India and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> east to <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. Increasing numbers are wintering in Australia. It hybridises with <i>H. r. tytleri</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Amur River">Amur River</a> area. It is thought that the two eastern Asia forms were once geographically separate, but the nest sites provided by expanding human habitation allowed the ranges to overlap.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <i>H. r. gutturalis</i> is a vagrant to <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sibley_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sibley-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> but is easily distinguished from the North American breeding subspecies, <i>H. r. erythrogaster</i>, by the latter's reddish underparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>H. r. tytleri</i>, first described by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_C._Jerdon" title="Thomas C. Jerdon">Thomas Jerdon</a> in 1864, and named for British soldier, naturalist and photographer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christopher_Tytler" title="Robert Christopher Tytler">Robert Christopher Tytler</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ssp_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssp-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> has deep orange-red underparts and an incomplete breast band. The tail is also longer.<sup id="cite_ref-rasmussen_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rasmussen-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> It breeds in central <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> south to northern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and winters from eastern <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> east to <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg/220px-BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg/330px-BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg/440px-BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1932" data-file-height="1288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BarnSwallow_cajay.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>H. r. erythrogaster</i> in Washington State, US</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>H. r. erythrogaster</i>, the North American subspecies described by <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Boddaert" title="Pieter Boddaert">Pieter Boddaert</a> in 1783,<sup id="cite_ref-ssp_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssp-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> differs from the European subspecies in having redder underparts and a narrower, often incomplete, blue breast band. It breeds throughout North America, from <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> to southern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, and migrates to the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a>, Costa Rica, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> and South America to winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A few may winter in the southernmost parts of the breeding range. This subspecies funnels through Central America on a narrow front and is therefore abundant on passage in the lowlands of both coasts.<sup id="cite_ref-Stiles_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stiles-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The short wings, red belly and incomplete breast band of <i>H. r. tytleri</i> are also found in <i>H. r. erythrogaster</i>, and <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> analyses show that barn swallows from North America colonised the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Baikal</a> region of Siberia, a dispersal direction opposite to that for most changes in distribution between North America and Eurasia.<sup id="cite_ref-zinkpavlova_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zinkpavlova-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat_and_range">Habitat and range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Habitat and range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barn_swallow_(Hirundo_rustica_rustica)_juveniles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_juveniles.jpg/220px-Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_juveniles.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_juveniles.jpg/330px-Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_juveniles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_juveniles.jpg/440px-Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_juveniles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2568" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barn_swallow_(Hirundo_rustica_rustica)_juveniles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>H. r. rustica</i> juveniles</div></div></div>

<p>The preferred habitat of the barn swallow is open country with low vegetation, such as pasture, meadows and farmland, preferably with nearby water. This swallow avoids heavily wooded or precipitous areas and densely built-up locations. The presence of accessible open structures such as barns, stables, or culverts to provide nesting sites, and exposed locations such as wires, roof ridges or bare branches for perching, are also important in the bird's selection of its breeding range.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It breeds in the Northern Hemisphere from sea level to typically 2,700&#160;m (8,900&#160;ft),<sup id="cite_ref-BirdLife_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdLife-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> but to 3,000&#160;m (9,800&#160;ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a><sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and North America,<sup id="cite_ref-umich_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umich-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and it is absent only from deserts and the cold northernmost parts of the continents. Over much of its range, it avoids towns, and in Europe is replaced in urban areas by the <a href="/wiki/Common_house_martin" title="Common house martin">house martin</a>. However, in <a href="/wiki/Honsh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Honshū">Honshū</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, the barn swallow is a more urban bird, with the <a href="/wiki/Red-rumped_swallow" title="Red-rumped swallow">red-rumped swallow</a> (<i>Cecropis daurica</i>) replacing it as the rural species.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In winter, the barn swallow is cosmopolitan in its choice of habitat, avoiding only dense forests and deserts.<sup id="cite_ref-SASOL_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SASOL-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> It is most common in open, low vegetation habitats, such as <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> and ranch land, and in <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, South Africa and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a> it is described as being particularly attracted to burnt or harvested <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> fields and the waste from the cane.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilty_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hilty-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mercy_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercy-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ffrench_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ffrench-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In the absence of suitable roost sites, they may sometimes roost on wires where they are more exposed to predators.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Individual birds tend to return to the same wintering locality each year<sup id="cite_ref-burton_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and congregate from a large area to <a href="/wiki/Communal_roosting" title="Communal roosting">roost</a> in reed beds.<sup id="cite_ref-mercy_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercy-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> These roosts can be extremely large; one in Nigeria had an estimated 1.5 million birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> These roosts are thought to be a protection from predators, and the arrival of roosting birds is synchronised in order to overwhelm predators like <a href="/wiki/African_hobby" title="African hobby">African hobbies</a>. The barn swallow has been recorded as breeding in the more temperate parts of its winter range, such as the mountains of <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and in central Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lekagul_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lekagul-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Migration of barn swallows between Britain and South Africa was first established on 23 December 1912 when a bird that had been ringed by James Masefield at a nest in Staffordshire, was found in Natal.<sup id="cite_ref-MigrationAtlas_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MigrationAtlas-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> As would be expected for a long-distance migrant, this bird has occurred as a vagrant to such distant areas as Hawaii, <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, Greenland, <a href="/wiki/Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Tristan da Cunha">Tristan da Cunha</a> the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and even Antarctica.<sup id="cite_ref-Korczak-Abshire_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korczak-Abshire-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg/220px-Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg/330px-Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg/440px-Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2169" data-file-height="1413" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Swallow_August_2013-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chicks in the nest</div></div></div>
<p>The barn swallow is similar in its habits to other aerial <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivores</a>, including other swallow species and the unrelated <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">swifts</a>. It is not a particularly fast flier, with a speed estimated at about 11 metres per second (25&#160;mph), up to 20 metres per second (45&#160;mph) and a wing beat rate of approximately 5, up to 7–9 times each second.<sup id="cite_ref-flight_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Park_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Park-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The barn swallow typically feeds in open areas<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> 7–8&#160;m (23–26&#160;ft) above shallow water or the ground often following animals, humans or farm machinery to catch disturbed insects, but it will occasionally pick prey items from the water surface, walls and plants.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In the breeding areas, large <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">flies</a> make up around 70% of the diet, with <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> also a significant component. However, in Europe, the barn swallow consumes fewer aphids than the <a href="/wiki/Common_house_martin" title="Common house martin">house</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sand_martin" title="Sand martin">sand martins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> On the wintering grounds, <a href="/wiki/Hymenoptera" title="Hymenoptera">Hymenoptera</a>, especially flying <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a>, are important food items.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> When egg-laying barn swallows hunt in pairs, but otherwise will form often <a href="/wiki/Group_size_measures" title="Group size measures">large flocks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The amount of food a clutch will get depends on the size of the clutch, with larger clutches getting more food on average. The timing of a clutch also determines the food given; later broods get food that is smaller in size compared to earlier broods. This is because larger insects are too far away from the nest to be profitable in terms of energy expenditure.<sup id="cite_ref-Waugh1978_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh1978-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">Isotope</a> studies have shown that wintering populations may utilise different feeding habitats, with British breeders feeding mostly over grassland, whereas <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> birds utilised woodland more.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Another study showed that a single population breeding in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> actually wintered in two separate areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Mollerhobson_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mollerhobson-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The barn swallow drinks by skimming low over lakes or rivers and scooping up water with its open mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-umich_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umich-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This bird bathes in a similar fashion, dipping into the water for an instant while in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-burton_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Swallows gather in communal roosts after breeding, sometimes thousands strong. Reed beds are regularly favoured, with the birds swirling <i>en masse</i> before swooping low over the reeds.<sup id="cite_ref-Mullarney_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullarney-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Reed beds are an important source of food prior to and whilst on migration; although the barn swallow is a diurnal migrant that can feed on the wing whilst it travels low over ground or water, the reed beds enable fat deposits to be established or replenished.<sup id="cite_ref-Euring_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euring-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG/220px-Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG/330px-Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG/440px-Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2519" data-file-height="1936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cr%C3%ADasHirundorustica.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four well grown chicks in a nest</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barn_swallow_(Hirundo_rustica_rustica)_singing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_singing.jpg/220px-Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_singing.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_singing.jpg/330px-Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_singing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_singing.jpg/440px-Barn_swallow_%28Hirundo_rustica_rustica%29_singing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3137" data-file-height="2091" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barn_swallow_(Hirundo_rustica_rustica)_singing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>H. r. rustica</i> fledgling begging</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex,_England_-chick-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex%2C_England_-chick-8.jpg/220px-Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex%2C_England_-chick-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex%2C_England_-chick-8.jpg/330px-Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex%2C_England_-chick-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex%2C_England_-chick-8.jpg/440px-Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex%2C_England_-chick-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3931" data-file-height="3359" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hirundo_rustica_-West_Sussex,_England_-chick-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile bird in Sussex</div></div></div>
<p>The male barn swallow returns to the breeding grounds before the females and selects a nest site, which is then advertised to females with a circling flight and song.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Plumage may be used to advertise: in some populations, like in the subspecies <i>H. r. gutturalis</i>, darker ventral plumage in males is associated with higher breeding success. In other populations,<sup id="cite_ref-LiuScordato2018_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiuScordato2018-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> the breeding success of the male is related to the length of the tail streamers, with longer streamers being more attractive to the female.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saino_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saino-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Males with longer tail feathers are generally longer-lived and more disease resistant, females thus gaining an indirect fitness benefit from this form of selection, since longer tail feathers indicate a genetically stronger individual which will produce offspring with enhanced vitality.<sup id="cite_ref-Moller_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moller-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Males in northern Europe have longer tails than those further south; whereas in Spain the male's tail streamers are only 5% longer than the female's, in Finland the difference is 20%. In Denmark, the average male tail length increased by 9% between 1984 and 2004, but it is possible that climatic changes may lead in the future to shorter tails if summers become hot and dry.<sup id="cite_ref-BB102_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB102-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Males with long streamers also have larger white tail spots, and since feather-eating <a href="/wiki/Chewing_louse" class="mw-redirect" title="Chewing louse">bird lice</a> prefer white feathers, large white tail spots without <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasite</a> damage again demonstrate breeding quality; there is a positive association between spot size and the number of offspring produced each season.<sup id="cite_ref-Kose_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kose-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The breeding season of the barn swallow is variable; in the southern part of the range, the breeding season usually is from February or March to early to mid September, although some late second and third broods finish in October. In the northern part of the range, it usually starts late May to early June and ends the same time as the breeding season of the southernmost birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2010_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2010-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both sexes defend the nest, but the male is particularly aggressive and territorial.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Once established, pairs stay together to breed for life, but extra-pair <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">copulation</a> is common, making this species <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetically</a> <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">polygamous</a>, despite being socially <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamous</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Males guard females actively to avoid being <a href="/wiki/Cuckold" title="Cuckold">cuckolded</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Males may use deceptive alarm calls to disrupt extrapair copulation attempts toward their mates.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As its name implies, the barn swallow typically nests inside accessible buildings such as barns and stables, or under bridges and wharves.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffin_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffin-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Before man-made sites became common, it nested on cliff faces or in caves, but this is now rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The neat cup-shaped nest is placed on a beam or against a suitable vertical projection. It is constructed by both sexes, although more often by the female, with mud pellets collected in their beaks and lined with grasses, feathers, algae<sup id="cite_ref-Duffin_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffin-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> or other soft materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The nest building ability of the male is also sexually selected; females will lay more eggs and at an earlier date with males who are better at nest construction, with the opposite being true with males that are not.<sup id="cite_ref-SolerCuervo1998_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolerCuervo1998-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> After building the nest, barn swallows may nest colonially where sufficient high-quality nest sites are available, and within a colony, each pair defends a territory around the nest which, for the European subspecies, is 4 to 8&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (43 to 86&#160;sq&#160;ft) in size. <a href="/wiki/Group_size_measures" title="Group size measures">Colony size</a> tends to be larger in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-umich_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umich-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America at least, barn swallows frequently engage in a <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualist</a> relationship with <a href="/wiki/Osprey" title="Osprey">ospreys</a>. Barn swallows will build their nest below an osprey nest, receiving protection from other birds of prey that are repelled by the exclusively fish-eating ospreys. The ospreys are alerted to the presence of these predators by the alarm calls of the swallows.<sup id="cite_ref-umich_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umich-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are normally two broods, with the original nest being reused for the second brood and being repaired and reused in subsequent years. The female lays two to seven, but typically four or five, reddish-spotted white eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The clutch size is influenced by latitude, with clutch sizes of northern populations being higher on average than southern populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Moller1984_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moller1984-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are 20&#160;mm ×&#160;14&#160;mm (0.79&#160;in ×&#160;0.55&#160;in) in size, and weigh 1.9&#160;g (0.067&#160;oz), of which 5% is shell. In Europe, the female does almost all the <a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian incubation">incubation</a>, but in North America the male may incubate up to 25% of the time. The incubation period is normally 14–19 days, with another 18–23 days before the <a href="/wiki/Altricial" class="mw-redirect" title="Altricial">altricial</a> chicks fledge. The fledged young stay with, and are fed by, the parents for about a week after leaving the nest. Occasionally, first-year birds from the first brood will assist in feeding the second brood.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Compared to those from early broods, juvenile barn swallows from late broods have been found to migrate at a younger age, fuel less efficiently during migration and have lower return rates the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The barn swallow will <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Mobbing behavior">mob</a> intruders such as cats or <a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">accipiters</a> that venture too close to their nest, often flying very close to the threat.<sup id="cite_ref-Moller_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moller-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Adult barn swallows have few predators, but some are taken by accipiters, <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a>. <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">Brood parasitism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cowbird" title="Cowbird">cowbirds</a> in North America or <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo" title="Cuckoo">cuckoos</a> in Eurasia is rare.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-umich_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umich-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hatching success is 90% and the fledging survival rate is 70–90%. Average mortality is 70–80% in the first year and 40–70% for the adult. Although the record age is more than 11 years, most survive less than four years.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Barn swallow nestlings have prominent red gapes, a feature shown to induce feeding by parent birds. An experiment in manipulating brood size and immune system showed the vividness of the gape was positively correlated with T-cell–mediated immunocompetence, and that larger brood size and injection with an antigen led to a less vivid gape.<sup id="cite_ref-Saino03_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saino03-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The barn swallow has been recorded as <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridising</a> with the <a href="/wiki/American_cliff_swallow" class="mw-redirect" title="American cliff swallow">cliff swallow</a> (<i>Petrochelidon pyrrhonota</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Cave_swallow" title="Cave swallow">cave swallow</a> (<i>P. fulva</i>) in North America, and the house martin (<i>Delichon urbicum</i>) in Eurasia, the cross with the latter being one of the most common passerine hybrids.<sup id="cite_ref-Moller_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moller-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:29.5px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG/120px-Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG" decoding="async" width="120" height="91" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG/180px-Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG/240px-Hirundo_rustica_14105.JPG 2x" data-file-width="928" data-file-height="700" /></a></div></div>

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>

			</div>

</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites_and_predators">Parasites and predators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Parasites and predators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Feather_hole.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Feather_hole.jpg/220px-Feather_hole.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Feather_hole.jpg/330px-Feather_hole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Feather_hole.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feather_hole.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Feeding trace of <i>Brueelia</i> lice on a tail feather</div></div></div>
<p>Barn swallows (and other small <a href="/wiki/Passerines" class="mw-redirect" title="Passerines">passerines</a>) often have characteristic <a href="/wiki/Feather_holes" class="mw-redirect" title="Feather holes">feather holes</a> on their wing and tail feathers. These holes were suggested as being caused by <a href="/wiki/Phthiraptera" class="mw-redirect" title="Phthiraptera">avian lice</a> such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Machaerilaemus_malleus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Machaerilaemus malleus (page does not exist)">Machaerilaemus malleus</a></i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Myrsidea_rustica&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Myrsidea rustica (page does not exist)">Myrsidea rustica</a></i>, although other studies suggest that they are mainly caused by species of <i><a href="/wiki/Brueelia" title="Brueelia">Brueelia</a></i>. Several other species of lice have been described from barn swallow hosts, including <i>Brueelia domestica</i> and <i>Philopterus microsomaticus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The avian lice prefer to feed on white tail spots, and they are generally found more numerously on short-tailed males, indicating the function of unbroken white tail spots as a measure of quality.<sup id="cite_ref-KoseMøller1999_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KoseMøller1999-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In Texas, the swallow bug (<i><a href="/wiki/Oeciacus_vicarius" title="Oeciacus vicarius">Oeciacus vicarius</a></i>), which is common on species such as the <a href="/wiki/Cliff_swallow" title="Cliff swallow">cliff swallow</a>, is also known to infest barn swallows.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Predatory bats such as the <a href="/wiki/Greater_false_vampire_bat" title="Greater false vampire bat">greater false vampire bat</a> are known to prey on barn swallows.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Swallows at their communal roosts attract predators and several falcon species make use of these opportunities. Falcon species confirmed as predators include the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/African_hobby" title="African hobby">African hobby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bijlsma_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijlsma-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The barn swallow has an enormous range, with an estimated global extent of 51,700,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (20,000,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) and a population of 190 million individuals. The species is evaluated as <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> on the 2007 <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and has no special status under the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (<a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a>), which regulates international trade in specimens of wild animals and plants.<sup id="cite_ref-umich_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-umich-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This is a species that has greatly benefited historically from forest clearance, which has created the open habitats it prefers, and from human habitation, which have given it an abundance of safe man-made nest sites. There have been local declines due to the use of <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> in Israel in the 1950s, competition for nest sites with <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a> in the US in the 19th century, and an ongoing gradual decline in numbers in parts of Europe and Asia due to agricultural intensification, reducing the availability of insect food. However, there has been an increase in the population in North America during the 20th century with the greater availability of nesting sites and subsequent range expansion, including the colonisation of northern <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner_2-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A specific threat to wintering birds from the European populations is the transformation by the South African government of a light aircraft runway near <a href="/wiki/Durban" title="Durban">Durban</a> into <a href="/wiki/King_Shaka_International_Airport" title="King Shaka International Airport">an international airport</a> for the <a href="/wiki/2010_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2010 FIFA World Cup">2010 FIFA World Cup</a>. The roughly 250&#160;m (270&#160;yd) square Mount Moreland reed bed is a night roost for more than three million barn swallows, which represent 1% of the global population and 8% of the European breeding population. The reed bed lies on the flight path of aircraft using the proposed La Mercy airport, and there were fears that it would be cleared because the birds could threaten aircraft safety.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BirdLife2_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdLife2-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> However, following detailed evaluation, advanced radar technology will be installed to enable planes using the airport to be warned of bird movements and, if necessary, take appropriate measures to avoid the flocks.<sup id="cite_ref-mercy_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mercy-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> may affect the barn swallow; drought causes weight loss and slow feather regrowth, and the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> will make it a more formidable obstacle for migrating European birds. Hot dry summers will reduce the availability of insect food for chicks. Conversely, warmer springs may lengthen the breeding season and result in more chicks, and the opportunity to use nest sites outside buildings in the north of the range might also lead to more offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-BB102_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB102-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nederlandsche_vogelen_(KB)_-_Hirundo_rustica_(030b).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Hirundo_rustica_%28030b%29.jpg/220px-Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Hirundo_rustica_%28030b%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="336" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Hirundo_rustica_%28030b%29.jpg/330px-Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Hirundo_rustica_%28030b%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Hirundo_rustica_%28030b%29.jpg/440px-Nederlandsche_vogelen_%28KB%29_-_Hirundo_rustica_%28030b%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="1342" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nederlandsche_vogelen_(KB)_-_Hirundo_rustica_(030b).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <i><a href="/wiki/Nederlandsche_Vogelen" class="mw-redirect" title="Nederlandsche Vogelen">Nederlandsche Vogelen</a></i> (1770)</div></div></div>
<p>The barn swallow is an attractive bird that feeds on flying insects and has therefore been tolerated by humans when it shares their buildings for nesting. As one of the earlier migrants, this conspicuous species is also seen as an early sign of summer's approach.<sup id="cite_ref-Welldon_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welldon-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, the barn swallow appears to have used man-made structures and bridges since time immemorial. An early reference is in <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Georgics" title="Georgics">Georgics</a></i> (29 BC), "<i>Ante garrula quam tignis nidum suspendat hirundo</i>" (Before the twittering swallow hangs its nest from the rafters).<sup id="cite_ref-Virgil_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Virgil-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many cattle farmers believed that swallows spread <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> infections, however a study in Sweden showed no evidence of the birds being reservoirs of the bacteria.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_literature">In literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many literary references are based on the barn swallow's northward migration as a symbol of spring or summer. The <a href="/wiki/Proverb" title="Proverb">proverb</a> about the necessity for more than one piece of evidence goes back at least to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>: "For as one swallow or one day does not make a spring, so one day or a short time does not make a fortunate or happy man."<sup id="cite_ref-Welldon_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welldon-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The barn swallow symbolises the coming of spring and thus love in the <i><a href="/wiki/Pervigilium_Veneris" title="Pervigilium Veneris">Pervigilium Veneris</a></i>, a late <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> poem. In his poem "<a href="/wiki/The_Waste_Land" title="The Waste Land">The Waste Land</a>", <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a> quoted the line <i>"Quando fiam uti chelidon [ut tacere desinam]?"</i> ("When will I be like the swallow, so that I can stop being silent?") This refers to the myth of <a href="/wiki/Philomela_(princess_of_Athens)" class="mw-redirect" title="Philomela (princess of Athens)">Philomela</a> in which she turns into a <a href="/wiki/Nightingale" class="mw-redirect" title="Nightingale">nightingale</a>, and her sister <a href="/wiki/Procne" title="Procne">Procne</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, an image of the assembly of swallows for their southward migration concludes <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">John Keats</a>'s ode "<a href="/wiki/To_Autumn" title="To Autumn">To Autumn</a>".
</p><p>The swallow is cited in several of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s plays for the swiftness of its flight, with "True hope is swift, and flies with swallow's wings" from Act 5 of <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_III_(play)" title="Richard III (play)">Richard III</a></i>, and "I have horse will follow where the game Makes way, and run like swallows o'er the plain." from the second act of <i><a href="/wiki/Titus_Andronicus" title="Titus Andronicus">Titus Andronicus</a></i>. Shakespeare references the annual migration of the species in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Winter%27s_Tale" title="The Winter&#39;s Tale">The Winter's Tale</a></i>, Act 4: "Daffodils, That come before the swallow dares, and take The winds of March with beauty".
</p><p>A swallow is the main character in <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>'s story, <a href="/wiki/The_Happy_Prince_and_Other_Tales" title="The Happy Prince and Other Tales">The Happy Prince</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Birds_in_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds in culture">Birds in culture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gilbert_White" title="Gilbert White">Gilbert White</a> studied the barn swallow in detail in his pioneering work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_History_of_Selborne" class="mw-redirect" title="The Natural History of Selborne">The Natural History of Selborne</a></i>, but even this careful observer was uncertain whether it migrated or hibernated in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-white_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere, its long journeys have been well observed, and a <a href="/wiki/Swallow_tattoo" title="Swallow tattoo">swallow tattoo</a> is popular amongst nautical men as a symbol of a safe return; the tradition was that a mariner had a tattoo of this fellow wanderer after sailing 5,000&#160;nmi (9,300&#160;km; 5,800&#160;mi). A second swallow would be added after 10,000&#160;nmi (19,000&#160;km; 12,000&#160;mi) at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-mmbc_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmbc-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In the past, the tolerance for this beneficial <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivore</a> was reinforced by superstitions regarding damage to the barn swallow's nest. Such an act might lead to cows giving bloody milk, or no milk at all, or to hens ceasing to lay.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This may be a factor in the longevity of swallows' nests. Survival, with suitable annual refurbishment, for 10–15 years is regular, and one nest was reported to have been occupied for 48 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is depicted as the <i>martlet</i>, <i>merlette</i> or <i>merlot</i> in <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a>, where it represents younger sons who have no lands. It is also represented as lacking feet as this was a common belief at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooper92_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper92-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> As a result of a campaign by <a href="/wiki/Ornithology" title="Ornithology">ornithologists</a>, the barn swallow has been the national bird of <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> since 23 June 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-Estonia_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estonia-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Estonia2_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estonia2-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Barn_swallows_on_postage_stamps">Barn swallows on postage stamps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Barn swallows on postage stamps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Barn swallows are one of the most depicted <a href="/wiki/Birds_on_stamps" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds on stamps">birds on postage stamps</a> around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Barn_swallow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
